<h1>Section 1: Welcome to StrictTS</h1>
<p>This is a <strong>bold</strong> statement about <em>compiling</em> TypeScript to native code.</p>
<h2>Why Native Compilation?</h2>
<p>The idea is simple: if you have <strong>type information</strong> at compile time, you can emit</p>
<p><code>machine code</code> instead of interpreting through V8.</p>
<h3>The Trade-offs</h3>
<p>Here is a <a href="https://example.com/spec">link to the spec</a> for reference.</p>
<p>Some things you lose:</p>
<p>- Dynamic property access</p>
<p>- The <code>eval</code> function</p>
<p>- Runtime type changes</p>
<pre><code>function add(a: number, b: number): number {
  return a + b
}</code></pre>
<h2>Performance</h2>
<p>With <strong>strict types</strong> and no dynamic features, the compiler knows the exact</p>
<p>memory layout of every object. This means:</p>
<h3>No Boxing</h3>
<p>Numbers are just <code>double</code> values on the stack. No heap allocation.</p>
<h3>No Garbage Collection Pressure</h3>
<p>With arena allocation, we free everything at once at the end.</p>
<h2>Conclusion</h2>
<p>This is a <em>real</em> markdown document with <strong>bold</strong>, <em>italic</em>, <code>code</code>,</p>
<p><a href="https://example.com">links</a>, and code blocks. It exercises the full</p>
<p>parser pipeline.</p>
<h3>Final Thoughts</h3>
<p>The gap between TypeScript and a <strong>compilable language</strong> is smaller than</p>
<p>most people think. We just need to <em>cut the right corners</em>.</p>
<p>---</p>

<p>most people think. We just need to <em>cut the right corners</em>.</p>
<p>---</p>
<h1>Section 16: Welcome to StrictTS</h1>
<p>This is a <strong>bold</strong> statement about <em>compiling</em> TypeScript to native code.</p>
<h2>Why Native Compilation?</h2>
<p>The idea is simple: if you have <strong>type information</strong> at compile time, you can emit</p>
<p><code>machine code</code> instead of interpreting through V8.</p>
<h3>The Trade-offs</h3>
<p>Here is a <a href="https://example.com/spec">link to the spec</a> for reference.</p>
<p>Some things you lose:</p>
<p>- Dynamic property access</p>
<p>- The <code>eval</code> function</p>
<p>- Runtime type changes</p>
<pre><code>function add(a: number, b: number): number {
  return a + b
}</code></pre>
<h2>Performance</h2>
<p>With <strong>strict types</strong> and no dynamic features, the compiler knows the exact</p>
<p>memory layout of every object. This means:</p>
<h3>No Boxing</h3>
<p>Numbers are just <code>double</code> values on the stack. No heap allocation.</p>
<h3>No Garbage Collection Pressure</h3>
<p>With arena allocation, we free everything at once at the end.</p>
<h2>Conclusion</h2>
<p>This is a <em>real</em> markdown document with <strong>bold</strong>, <em>italic</em>, <code>code</code>,</p>
<p><a href="https://example.com">links</a>, and code blocks. It exercises the full</p>
<p>parser pipeline.</p>

<p>The gap between TypeScript and a <strong>compilable language</strong> is smaller than</p>
<p>most people think. We just need to <em>cut the right corners</em>.</p>

<h1>Section 31: Welcome to StrictTS</h1>
<p>This is a <strong>bold</strong> statement about <em>compiling</em> TypeScript to native code.</p>
<h2>Why Native Compilation?</h2>
<p>The idea is simple: if you have <strong>type information</strong> at compile time, you can emit</p>
<p><code>machine code</code> instead of interpreting through V8.</p>
<h3>The Trade-offs</h3>
<p>Here is a <a href="https://example.com/spec">link to the spec</a> for reference.</p>
<p>Some things you lose:</p>
<p>- Dynamic property access</p>
<p>- The <code>eval</code> function</p>
<p>- Runtime type changes</p>
<pre><code>function add(a: number, b: number): number {
  return a + b
}</code></pre>
<h2>Performance</h2>
<p>With <strong>strict types</strong> and no dynamic features, the compiler knows the exact</p>
<p>memory layout of every object. This means:</p>
<h3>No Boxing</h3>
<p>Numbers are just <code>double</code> values on the stack. No heap allocation.</p>
<h3>No Garbage Collection Pressure</h3>
<p>With arena allocation, we free everything at once at the end.</p>
<h2>Conclusion</h2>
<p>This is a <em>real</em> markdown document with <strong>bold</strong>, <em>italic</em>, <code>code</code>,</p>

<p>parser pipeline.</p>

<p>memory layout of every object. This means:</p>
<h3>No Boxing</h3>
<p>Numbers are just <code>double</code> values on the stack. No heap allocation.</p>
<h3>No Garbage Collection Pressure</h3>
<p>With arena allocation, we free everything at once at the end.</p>

<h3>No Garbage Collection Pressure</h3>
<p>With arena allocation, we free everything at once at the end.</p>

<p>- The <code>eval</code> function</p>
<p>- Runtime type changes</p>
<pre><code>function add(a: number, b: number): number {
  return a + b
}</code></pre>
<h2>Performance</h2>
<p>With <strong>strict types</strong> and no dynamic features, the compiler knows the exact</p>
<p>memory layout of every object. This means:</p>
<h3>No Boxing</h3>
<p>Numbers are just <code>double</code> values on the stack. No heap allocation.</p>
<h3>No Garbage Collection Pressure</h3>
<p>With arena allocation, we free everything at once at the end.</p>

<p><code>machine code</code> instead of interpreting through V8.</p>
<h3>The Trade-offs</h3>
<p>Here is a <a href="https://example.com/spec">link to the spec</a> for reference.</p>
<p>Some things you lose:</p>
<p>- Dynamic property access</p>
<p>- The <code>eval</code> function</p>
<p>- Runtime type changes</p>
<pre><code>function add(a: number, b: number): number {
  return a + b
}</code></pre>
<h2>Performance</h2>
<p>With <strong>strict types</strong> and no dynamic features, the compiler knows the exact</p>
<p>memory layout of every object. This means:</p>
<h3>No Boxing</h3>
<p>Numbers are just <code>double</code> values on the stack. No heap allocation.</p>
<h3>No Garbage Collection Pressure</h3>
<p>With arena allocation, we free everything at once at the end.</p>
<h2>Conclusion</h2>
<p>This is a <em>real</em> markdown document with <strong>bold</strong>, <em>italic</em>, <code>code</code>,</p>
<p><a href="https://example.com">links</a>, and code blocks. It exercises the full</p>
<p>parser pipeline.</p>
<h3>Final Thoughts</h3>
<p>The gap between TypeScript and a <strong>compilable language</strong> is smaller than</p>
<p>most people think. We just need to <em>cut the right corners</em>.</p>

<p>This is a <strong>bold</strong> statement about <em>compiling</em> TypeScript to native code.</p>
<h2>Why Native Compilation?</h2>
<p>The idea is simple: if you have <strong>type information</strong> at compile time, you can emit</p>
<p><code>machine code</code> instead of interpreting through V8.</p>
<h3>The Trade-offs</h3>
<p>Here is a <a href="https://example.com/spec">link to the spec</a> for reference.</p>
<p>Some things you lose:</p>
<p>- Dynamic property access</p>
<p>- The <code>eval</code> function</p>
<p>- Runtime type changes</p>
<pre><code>function add(a: number, b: number): number {
  return a + b
}</code></pre>
<h2>Performance</h2>
<p>With <strong>strict types</strong> and no dynamic features, the compiler knows the exact</p>
<p>memory layout of every object. This means:</p>
<h3>No Boxing</h3>
<p>Numbers are just <code>double</code> values on the stack. No heap allocation.</p>
<h3>No Garbage Collection Pressure</h3>
<p>With arena allocation, we free everything at once at the end.</p>
<h2>Conclusion</h2>
<p>This is a <em>real</em> markdown document with <strong>bold</strong>, <em>italic</em>, <code>code</code>,</p>
<p><a href="https://example.com">links</a>, and code blocks. It exercises the full</p>
<p>parser pipeline.</p>
<h3>Final Thoughts</h3>
<p>The gap between TypeScript and a <strong>compilable language</strong> is smaller than</p>
<p>most people think. We just need to <em>cut the right corners</em>.</p>

<p>most people think. We just need to <em>cut the right corners</em>.</p>
<p>---</p>
<h1>Section 135: Welcome to StrictTS</h1>
<p>This is a <strong>bold</strong> statement about <em>compiling</em> TypeScript to native code.</p>
<h2>Why Native Compilation?</h2>
<p>The idea is simple: if you have <strong>type information</strong> at compile time, you can emit</p>
<p><code>machine code</code> instead of interpreting through V8.</p>
<h3>The Trade-offs</h3>
<p>Here is a <a href="https://example.com/spec">link to the spec</a> for reference.</p>
<p>Some things you lose:</p>
<p>- Dynamic property access</p>
<p>- The <code>eval</code> function</p>
<p>- Runtime type changes</p>
<pre><code>function add(a: number, b: number): number {
  return a + b
}</code></pre>
<h2>Performance</h2>
<p>With <strong>strict types</strong> and no dynamic features, the compiler knows the exact</p>
<p>memory layout of every object. This means:</p>
<h3>No Boxing</h3>
<p>Numbers are just <code>double</code> values on the stack. No heap allocation.</p>
<h3>No Garbage Collection Pressure</h3>
<p>With arena allocation, we free everything at once at the end.</p>
<h2>Conclusion</h2>
<p>This is a <em>real</em> markdown document with <strong>bold</strong>, <em>italic</em>, <code>code</code>,</p>
<p><a href="https://example.com">links</a>, and code blocks. It exercises the full</p>
<p>parser pipeline.</p>

<p>most people think. We just need to <em>cut the right corners</em>.</p>

<h1>Section 150: Welcome to StrictTS</h1>
<p>This is a <strong>bold</strong> statement about <em>compiling</em> TypeScript to native code.</p>
<h2>Why Native Compilation?</h2>
<p>The idea is simple: if you have <strong>type information</strong> at compile time, you can emit</p>
<p><code>machine code</code> instead of interpreting through V8.</p>
<h3>The Trade-offs</h3>
<p>Here is a <a href="https://example.com/spec">link to the spec</a> for reference.</p>
<p>Some things you lose:</p>
<p>- Dynamic property access</p>
<p>- The <code>eval</code> function</p>
<p>- Runtime type changes</p>
<pre><code>function add(a: number, b: number): number {
  return a + b
}</code></pre>
<h2>Performance</h2>
<p>With <strong>strict types</strong> and no dynamic features, the compiler knows the exact</p>
<p>memory layout of every object. This means:</p>
<h3>No Boxing</h3>
<p>Numbers are just <code>double</code> values on the stack. No heap allocation.</p>
<h3>No Garbage Collection Pressure</h3>
<p>With arena allocation, we free everything at once at the end.</p>
<h2>Conclusion</h2>
<p>This is a <em>real</em> markdown document with <strong>bold</strong>, <em>italic</em>, <code>code</code>,</p>
<p><a href="https://example.com">links</a>, and code blocks. It exercises the full</p>
<p>parser pipeline.</p>

<p>most people think. We just need to <em>cut the right corners</em>.</p>

<p>memory layout of every object. This means:</p>
<h3>No Boxing</h3>
<p>Numbers are just <code>double</code> values on the stack. No heap allocation.</p>
<h3>No Garbage Collection Pressure</h3>
<p>With arena allocation, we free everything at once at the end.</p>

<pre><code>function add(a: number, b: number): number {
  return a + b
}</code></pre>
<h2>Performance</h2>
<p>With <strong>strict types</strong> and no dynamic features, the compiler knows the exact</p>
<p>memory layout of every object. This means:</p>
<h3>No Boxing</h3>
<p>Numbers are just <code>double</code> values on the stack. No heap allocation.</p>
<h3>No Garbage Collection Pressure</h3>
<p>With arena allocation, we free everything at once at the end.</p>
